It is a great love story like \\"The Notebook\\", but also shows the military aspect of how many service members deal with their experiences and life after war like \\"Dear John\\". This novel shows how friends can influence decisions that we ourselves would not have made otherwise and how love can be found in the most unusual places.<br /> Initially, the story seems a little farfetched because how can someone seek a complete stranger out simply because he is lead to believe that the stranger's picture is his lucky charm. I think that any military member will tell you that a simple picture can make a difference when they are placed in a war zone and that is the only thing that they have that is close to their heart. In the novel \\"The Lucky One\\", a prior Marine named Thibault seeks out a woman whom he has never met simply because he finds her picture in Kuwait and then somehow survives several missions in Iraq. This alone does not influence Thibault's decision to seek Elizabeth out, but when his close friend is killed in a boating accident in front of him, it changes everything. I think that one situation changed Thibault even more than all the experiences from war and several deployments. Thibault follows his friend Victor's advice to seek the woman out to pay her back for simply \\"saving his life\\" through the many deployments and missions. The description of how Thibault walked from Colorado to North Carolina to find Elizabeth is amazing about how he walked through many obstacles with his dog Zeus simply to find her. This devotion and dedication alone would make anyone to know about how this man that is lost and in need of something he cannot put his finger on, find his way. This novel is not only about the love story that develops between Thibault and Elizabeth, but about how each of us as individuals require certain things to rediscover ourselves and who we are. Thibault figures out how to deal more with the loss of his friend and Elizabeth finally discovers what she needs to become herself again after many years. Sparks shows that after the discovery of why Thibault is actually in Hampton, Elizabeth is reminded of her brother and his strange attachment to that very picture. Thibault feels he needs to keep Elizabeth and her son safe from her ex-husband since he \\"owes\\" her his life. The ending is very surprising how the picture eventually keeps Elizabeth's son safe, but allows for the \\"bad guy\\" to be removed from the story for a happily ever after ending. I think the way Sparks describes this situation and the events that follow, makes the reader feel like it could actually happen. The way Sparks describes how love can come out of a strange situation and overcome many things, makes any reader feel that love does conquer all.<br /> The main idea about a man seeking a woman in a picture he found and is lead to believe is his lucky charm seems a little strange, but Sparks does a wonderful job making me feel it could actually happen. I think that anyone that is put in a very stressful situation such as war can be lead to believe that a simple picture could be a lucky charm and keep them safe. Thibault's walk from Colorado to North Carolina is strange, but given the situation can be found normal as a form of therapy to help him cope with the loss of his friend. Sparks does a great job showing how love can easily develop when two lonely people finally find that person to share silence with and is easy to talk to. He also shows how many service members today deal with many things post war that most of us that make up the population can never fully understand. I think this book is a great addition to the Nicholas Sparks collection and should be read by those that love his books and new readers that love a good love story.

B003LL3NOK|4.0|It's truly a sign that when George Romero can no longer make a terrific zombie film anymore that this whole zombie craze B.S. that's swept the world in the last 8 years or so, is....well, dead.  A friend of mine summed it up by saying that there should be a law passed by congress that no zombie films can be produced, in Hollywood(especially) or independently, for at least a decade.  With zombies in T.V commercials, every video game including a \\"zombie mode\\", eight billion zombie novels appearing on the shelves(including those atrocious cutesy zombie survival guides), a television show(which really is much better than I thought it would be oddly enough), and even making appearances on Spongebob Squarepants, how can it not all fall into parody?  Every zombie film that comes out anymore seems to be a comedy!  How do you bring the creepiness or seriousness back to the genre?  Zombie movies have always been a kind of taboo, gruesome movies that only weird horror people watched.  I have no problem with the zombie film getting more exposure and becoming more acceptable, but it seems that whenever that happens to a genre or idea, it soon becomes overkill and almost immediately turns to crap, especially when Hollywood goes in for a piece of the action.<br />Sorry, I didn't intend to rant about all this for so long, but I can't help it when zombie movies are the hot topic.<br />So, what's with this Romero movie everybody's bitching about?  Well, I gotta say that Survival of the Dead isn't quite as bad as it's reputation would have you believe.  The problem is that it's Romero.  We count on Romero to give us the great zombie film that will stomp all the wannabees to dust, to show us all how it's REALLY done.  Problem is, is that he's buried in a sea of his own imitators, most of them bad.  And Land of the Dead, Diary and Survival come across to horror fans like Lucas' last Three Star Wars movies come across to Sci-Fi fans.  What could Romero possibly do to follow up three classic films, especially after such a hiatus that allowed their legacy to grow.<br />The sad fact is that Survival comes across like a straight-to-video or made-for-SyFy movie.  An above average one, in my opinion, but a run of the mill one nonetheless, and that's not what we expect from Romero.<br />The story follows a rogue group of soldiers looking for their own private piece of the world, and think they've found it in a New England coastal island, but a Hatfield/McCoy-ish civil war is going on there.  One family, led by Kenneth Welsh(who I loved in Twin Peaks, and is a dead ringer for Terence Stamp) wants to wipe all zombies out and make the island a safehaven, while the opposing family are religious nuts who want to keep the dead \\"alive\\" and chained.  Why?  To try to get them to acquire an appetite for another meat besides human.  The zombies don't seem to comply when horses and other animals are served to them, so the zombies spend much of their time doing manual labor and chores around the farm.  It's an extension on ideas put forth in Day of the Dead.<br />Once the soldiers and Welsh(who had been exiled) come to the island, a war is waged.  Like I said before, I really don't think this movie was all that bad.  No, the cast isn't full of DeNiros, Romero has started using CG since Land(which is probably my biggest complaint as it makes it look all the more SyFy channel-ish, and is just a flat out sin amongst horror fans, right on par with the running zombies), the social message isn't as strong, gore is minimal, and a twist involving an identical twin sister will make you roll your eyes.<br />Even with all of that, I'll take this movie over 100 Zombielands and 1,000,000 Resident Evils. B007MDB71O|5.0|Without Burroughs' original John Carter novels, much of today's science-fiction genre wouldn't exist.<br />That said, pay no heed to the critics who complain that this is all stuff we've seen before in Star Wars and Avatar; Lucas and Cameron have both said that they were fans of the John Carter books, though I doubt either bothered to add that they also borrowed heavily from them for their own works. The bit I love is that they're claiming that the century-old John Carter series (the majority of the source-material for this film was originally published in 1912) borrows heavily from the 30+ years old Star Wars, and the much younger Avatar. If these critics aren't going to bother getting their facts straight before going to publication, then I believe that we shouldn't bother listening to them; why should we listen to the \\"intellectually-disadvantaged\\" when choosing our entertainment? Seriously, those folks need to be fired; trained gibbons on meth could do a better job than ANYONE who claims that the much older story borrows from the infinitely more recent. These people would say that the ancient Greeks stole a lot from 'Clash of the Titans' when they wrote the tales of Perseus, Hercules, etc.<br />Having never been engaged by the Avatar-hype, I haven't bothered to see it, and therefor can't point out the bits that Cameron lifted, but there are many immediately-obvious \\"Lucas-inspirations\\": Padwars, Jeddaks, etc.<br />The TRUTH is that Stanton did an incredible job of bundling up this initial installment of the Chronicles of Barsoom into a nice stand-alone story that appeals better to 21st Century societal mores than a literal interpretation would have done, and in so doing, fails to tread too harshly on Burroughs' toes. The biggest 'For Instance' that leaps to mind would be that the Barsoomians in the movie wear clothes; in Burroughs' books, they wore only those nifty leather and steel harnesses. Additionally, the screen-writers gave John Carter a little more back-story to make him a slightly more 3 dimensional character.<br />I love the books, I loved the movie, and I'm horrified at the treatment it has received from the Critics that worked so effortlessly to make this masterpiece a stand-alone story; the John Carter/Chronicles of Barsoom would have made Disney a MINT if they hadn't so thoroughly botched the marketing campaign.<br />Buy this movie - chances are very good that you'll enjoy it thoroughly, unless you prefer to have the gibbons choose what you like and don't like...story to make him a slightly more 3 dimensional character.<br />I love the books, I loved the movie, and I'm horrified at the treatment it has received from the Critics that worked so effortlessly to make this masterpiece a stand-alone story; the John Carter/Chronicles of Barsoom would have made Disney a MINT if they hadn't so thoroughly botched the marketing campaign.<br />Buy this movie - chances are very good that you'll enjoy it thoroughly, unless you prefer to have the gibbons choose what you like and don't like...

B000002C1V|3.0|This is a dreary, depressing, self indulgent CD but it definitely has it moments.<br /><br />At the time there was a strong sense that Roger Waters was taking the opportunity to stick the knife into Pink Floyd and give it a little twist. It certainly guaranteed the bitter rift between Waters and Gilmour.<br /><br />I followed Pink Floyd from the earliest days and watched them in small clubs in London before they hit the big time. Pink Floyd's work is divided into clear phases. The first, with Syd, was psychedelic, electronic and was very typical of the era - nothing truly standout, but with a level of musical excellence that was uncommon in those raw, early days. I liked several of the instrumentals but had Syd Barrett not left Pink Floyd, no-one would remember them today.<br /><br />The second phase started with Dark Side and ran through to The Wall. The music was sophisticated, soulful, deep, and utterly inspired. Wish you were here, and Animals were theme oriented with many of the anthem quality songs that appeared with decreasing regularity right to the very end of Pink Floyd. This was the classic music of the progressive rock scene and no-one ever came close to emulating their mastery.<br /><br />The Wall was really the beginning of the end. Yes, it is much feted today, but has only four fundamental songs of any note, and a lot of musical dross. The Final Cut demonstrated just how out of touch Waters had become with the real world. The music is scrappy and the lyrics discordant with the times. Waters lambasts Thatcher at the very moment Britain was enjoying a brief, final resurgence of national pride in sending the Argentine invaders packing. It just felt wrong. If I met Waters at the time I would've said \\"you made your point with The Wall... the Final Cut just wasn't necessary\\".<br /><br />And that, fundamentally, was the end of Pink Floyd, skewered on the altar of Roger Waters ego, circling down to a hole in the ground. Curses.

0439023513|4.0|This review contains some spoilers.<br /><br />Overall I really enjoyed this trilogy, so much so that I read all three books in just a few days after seeing the Hunger Games film. The books are very well paced and the story always interesting. This is reflected in the four-star rating I have given to the book. On the whole I would give the series the highest marks.<br /><br />But I have to say, which seems to be the general consensus, that the way the series ended left me a bit disappointed. Collins created a very compelling story about survival and triumph against soul-crushing oppression, and more than anything created a very powerful, attractive character in Katniss Everdeen. This was a young woman who kept her family alive when her father died, who was endlessly resourceful and self-sufficient, who volunteered to save her sister from the Hunger Games, who triumphed against all odds in the arena while keeping her humanity and in doing so defying the all-powerful Capital, whose very example and decency would spark a revolution. Yet in the end it was like the author turned against her hero. I tore through the book waiting and waiting for some emotional payoff, for Katniss to finally achieve some measure of well deserved reward for all her trials, and it never happens. Katniss spends much of the final book emotionally crippled and hospitalized. Peeta is turned into a shell of his former self by the Capital who is programmed to hate the very sight of her. Katniss never gets to exact justice against Snow, the evil leader of the Capital. And Katniss is made to be just a pawn serving the whim of the District 13 leader, Coin, who is just as potentially evil. Topping it off, the author almost gratuitously kills off Katniss's sister. Not only did this seem like overkill, but it also takes away from Katniss's final act of heroism, assassinating Coin to protect the nation from trading one tyranny for another. What should have been an act of heroism is now transformed into anact of personal vengeance. Finally, what can we make of Katniss, having survived the dehumanizing cruelty of the arena twice, voting to subject the families of the Capital's leaders to the same cruelty? Maybe this was tactical, to disarm Coin as Katniss resolved to assassinate her, but it's done in such an ambiguous way that it needlessly chips away at Katniss's fundamental decency, the quality that for me drove the whole trilogy.<br /><br />I understand the need to balance fairy tales against realism, the horrors of war, the price of battling oppression, etc. It would have been unrealistic to have Katniss emerge completely unscathed from such an ordeal. But did the author really need to leave her scarred, emotionally crippled, abandoned and unheralded after all her heroism and sacrifices? In the end Katniss loses her home, her friends, her sister, her mother, Gale, is shot, burned, declared insane and is left living like a pariah in isolation in the charred remains of District 12, marrying the broken and equally discarded Peeta and living her life seemingly forgotten by the nation she saved. Did she even get a medal? Argue all you care to that there is some deeper philosophical meaning to all this. Maybe there is even a profound meaning in the reader being left to see the injustice of it all. But speaking as a card carrying member of the gritty realism school, forgive me for thinking that Katniss and the reader deserved a less depressing ending. Call me old-fashioned, but a main character as compelling as Katniss deserved a better fate. It is hardly giving in to the childish temptation of a fairy tale ending for the reader to ask for an ending that is less relentlessly grim towards the hero of the whole story.

0452277752|5.0|In newer editions of this collection, King's first Bachman Book, Rage, is omitted.  Stephen King took it out of print because of its similarity to the Columbine tragedy and the fact that at least one of the shooters cited it as an \\"influence\\".  I understand King's reluctance to keep the story in print, but I also believe everyone is responsible for their own actions, and that we can't blame art for the decisions people make.  For that reason, even though it's not as easy to find and may cost a little more, I recommend finding a copy of the original that has Rage as well as the other three novels.  Now, enough with the editorial.  Here are a few notes on each story.<br /><br />Rage--A brutally honest, if somewhat naive, look at what makes teenagers tick.  In public schools, a single classroom holds such a complex mixture of personalities, moods, and motivations, that it's a wonder schools are functional at all.  I find it interesting, though, that in today's society, the extreme insecurities and pressures the students in Rage operate under might be better suited to middle school than high school. Nonetheless, the senseless shootings stemming from social alienation proved prophetic, and the warning should not be ignored.<br /><br />The Long Walk--King predicts that our increasing bloodlust in entertainment will lead to a show where an audience watches a group of young men from poor families try to outwalk one another.  Everyone dies but the winner, who earns fame and fortune.  Needless to say, this is a bleak novel with a pessimistic view of humanity.<br /><br />Roadwork--I think this is the oddball of the group.  The protagonist is a man adrift, a middle-aged laundry worker who has to give up the house and workplace he cherishes so a new highway extension can be built right through them.  He falls into a deep depression, lies to his wife and bosses, and basically self-destructs.  After some alarming personal adventures, he decides to take his fate into his own hands.  In some ways, his journey reminds me of John Smith from The Dead Zone.<br /><br />The Running Man--Again, King looks into his crystal ball, and this time sees a future game show in which convicted violent criminals run from armed stalkers with, once again, fame and fortune as the prize for survival.  It's quite a bit different from the Arnold Schwarzenegger film that was based on it; it's much more dark and gritty.  Given the way it ends, I'm almost surprised King didn't pull it out of print along with Rage after another certain national tragedy, but I guess the circumstances were different enough not to merit doing that.<br /><br />These four novels have nothing whatsoever to do with the supernatural, as King was using his pseudonym early on as a vehicle to write and publish fiction outside of the expectations he had created under his own name.  Surprisingly, despite his other novels having monsters, ghosts, and demons, these stories have less mainstream appeal and seem more like a young author trying to be literary.  And achieving it, in my opinion.  All four of these books are dead serious, and delve deeply into their characters' psychological underpinnings.  Before he died from \\"cancer of the pseudonym,\\" as King calls it, Bachman did branch out into King territory with his next and last book, Thinner, but the early ones are in their own category.  For a different take on Stephen King's writing talents, and maybe an appreciation for his diversity and ambition as an author, give The Bachman Books a read.In some ways, his journey reminds me of John Smith from The Dead Zone.<br /><br />The Running Man--Again, King looks into his crystal ball, and this time sees a future game show in which convicted violent criminals run from armed stalkers with, once again, fame and fortune as the prize for survival.  It's quite a bit different from the Arnold Schwarzenegger film that was based on it; it's much more dark and gritty.  Given the way it ends, I'm almost surprised King didn't pull it out of print along with Rage after another certain national tragedy, but I guess the circumstances were different enough not to merit doing that.<br /><br />These four novels have nothing whatsoever to do with the supernatural, as King was using his pseudonym early on as a vehicle to write and publish fiction outside of the expectations he had created under his own name.  Surprisingly, despite his other novels having monsters, ghosts, and demons, these stories have less mainstream appeal and seem more like a young author trying to be literary.  And achieving it, in my opinion.  All four of these books are dead serious, and delve deeply into their characters' psychological underpinnings.  Before he died from \\"cancer of the pseudonym,\\" as King calls it, Bachman did branch out into King territory with his next and last book, Thinner, but the early ones are in their own category.  For a different take on Stephen King's writing talents, and maybe an appreciation for his diversity and ambition as an author, give The Bachman Books a read.

One, very quickly the viewer knows who the characters are - I mean really KNOWS who these people are.  Payne is able carry this through many of the supporting characters as well.  I don't know how he does it, but his characters are established to the viewer very soon after they first appear.  The other thing Payne does is create almost unbelievable situations and make them seem feasible.  If we saw them written in a story or saw them realized in less-adroit hands, some of his plotlines would be laughable - and not in a good way. In Payne's hands, we simply accept them because he makes them so plausable within the context of the storyline.<br /><br />One of the most genius moves was to place this story in Hawaii. Against a backdrop most would consider the destination of a lifetime, yes, the denizens have the same problems, the same heartbreaks, the same joys those of us living in less-stunning settings do.<br /><br />There are some movies that I enjoy as I would an old friend, welcoming them back again and again.  The Descendants is on its way of entering that elite group.

0316010669|3.0|Malcolm Gladwell's Blink is an otherwise very interesting book with a very serious flaw:  what he calls the Warren Harding Mistake.  Gladwell makes the  point that appearances can be deceiving, which most of us have experienced.  But this example of Warren Harding is not a good one.  Underlying his argument is an implied assumption that Harding was campaigning for President in the post 1960 television age.  In 1920, most Americans still lived on farms and got their news delivered through the mail.  There was no radio or television.  Harding campaigned from his front porch like a Gilded Age candidate.  It's doubtful that most voters even knew what he looked like.<br /><br />Harding was elected President in 1920 by 60 per cent of the electorate.  It was one of the largest vote majorities in American history.  His predecessor, Woodrow Wilson, had been elected and reelected by narrow pluralities and helped by a divided Republican party in 1912.  After being reelected in 1916, Wilson led the country into World War I that began with a burst of patriotism but ended in dubious European diplomatic machinations and in anger and disillusionment.<br /><br />Gladwell  mentions polls of historians that rank Harding as among the worst Presidents in American History. The poll of historians Gladwell is probably referring  to is the 1962 poll commissioned by Arthur Schlesinger, Sr.  President Kennedy was invited to participate because he was a published author of history,  Kennedy declined, because after one year in office, he said he understood the role events played in making or breaking a presidency.  Kennedy did comment, after seeing the results, he thought the poll had overrated Woodrow Wilson.<br /><br />What makes Harding's presidency the worst?  Well, no doubt his was the one where a foreign army invaded the United States and burned down the Capitol and the White House?  Wait a minute that was another President.<br /><br />The upshot of  Harding's presidency was this:  Harding died of congestive heart failure two years and five months into his Presidency,  just as the Teapot Dome scandal boiled over.  Coolidge assumed the Presidency upon Harding's death.  Coolidge appointed a bipartisan team of special prosecutors.  They indicted two cabinet officers.  One was convicted, the other acquitted. The acquitted cabinet officer was forced to resign by Coolidge.  Then he wrote a book blaming Harding for everything.  Harding was unavailable for comment.  Coolidge was elected to his own Presidency by 54 per cent of the vote in 1924.<br /><br />Where the historians saw idealism and nobility in Wilson, the electorate came to see megalomania and delusions of grandeur.  Where historians saw Harding as a small town, corrupt Babbitt (referring to the character in the eponymous Sinclair Lewis novel), the electorate saw a familiar gilded age politician who was probably corrupt but wasn't a threat to their life and limb.<br /><br />The real Warren G. Harding mistake here is in assuming that expertise is without bias.  I just don't agree that Harding is an example of looks deceiving the voters.  I think the voters knew exactly who Harding was and that was what they wanted.<br /><br />But Malcolm Gladwell is a very original thinker and the rest of Blink is well worth your time.g died of congestive heart failure two years and five months into his Presidency,  just as the Teapot Dome scandal boiled over. B0071XO8RA|5.0|Words can't describe how incredible this book is! This is easily the best book I've read so far this year, and I average about two books per week. Although this was originally a series of short stories, the Omnibus reads like one seamless storyline.<br /><br />I loved the character development in these stories. The people lead whole and fulfilling lives, experience an honest range of emotions (both good and bad,) closely guard secrets in their hearts, and constantly struggle for existence, to make sense of things, to achieve...despite living in a world comprised of only 140 floors.<br /><br />The world that Hugh Howey has constructed is so vast and rich, it's hard to remember that it's mainly set into the curved walls of a silo. All the systems required to have a functioning society; food production, IT, education, systems maintenance, rules on reproduction, and waste disposal, have been carefully planned out and accounted for. More importantly, the things that flesh out a society; taboos, religion, fears and hopes, the sound of children laughing are also painstakingly crafted by his words.<br /><br />The silo itself becomes a character. I love how Howey incorporates the stairs into the story. They become a test of will, a graceful arc of hope, or potential for despair. Not only is it the tie that connects the levels together, but it's also the gravity that keeps them apart. A barrier and a link. The very DNA of the silo's civilization.<br /><br />Just when you think this lovely combination of world-building AND character development couldn't get better, there's the writing. I especially liked how the artist masterfully weaved the metaphor of his title throughout the story. It was the element that could be crafted into a sweater that warms, but just as easily unraveled- like a lie. It's the truth being uncovered when the wool is pulled from one's eyes.<br /><br />Overall, I can't speak highly enough of this collection of short stories. At the end of the Kindle WoolOmnibus Edition (Wool #1-5) there is an interview with the author. He mentions that there will be more stories told in this world; I'm looking forward to reading all of them!

My parents played Ella Fitzgerald, Nat King Cole, Duke Ellington, Stan Getz, Gilberto & Jobim etc. What a kickstart! But wait for it: the first LP my mom bought me was what I still regard as the Fab 4s best: 'A hard day's night'. I soon went on to The Rolling Stones, Hendrix...and rode the entire expanding galaxy of hippie high musical gorgeousness that blossomed and bloomed during the 70s. The zeitgeist had to tire - it was just too frenetic and deliriously exhausting. It seemed that everything that could be done had been accomplished by 1979. And then came New Wave. God bless The Talking Heads, B52s, The Police, Nina Hagen punk etc. And then came the degradation of the 90s. Not to mention the 2000s. With some notable exceptions e.g. Smashing Pumpkins, U2 keeping the pot boiling, everything seemed derivative. Derivative. Nothing seemed new. Singers resorted to screaming (Nirvana and worse), metal went to death, sounding as if straight from hell itself. Serious singers felt it cool to sound all frail and feeble (REM) - and out of tune (Red House Painters). But nothing really new. Only new levels of degradation or feebleness.<br /><br />So a while ago I am surfing through the Rolling Stone 500 Greatest Album of all Times & The 100 Greatest Albums of the 2000s. I agree with Kid A at the top of the latter but in 2nd place is a group I never heard of (don't forget I am generally so disillusioned with more or less anything post c1989). Off to the best local CD shop but can't find 'Is this it' - only 'Angles'. So I buy it and...Kapow! Jackpot! Struck the gold! I have finally found a rock and roll band that has influences yet is not derivative, that has a new and so excitingly different sound. A group of highly accomplished musicians. Their song writing is brilliant genius. The vocals are strong, effortless, expressive, strikingly accomplished, the voice of maestro Casblancas actually an additional instrument! I now have 'Is this it' and love it too - 4 star. Then I got 'First impressions of earth' - 5 star fosho. As with angles - 5 star. Fosho!!!<br /><br />Angles is so clever - the band plays around with various styles of rock music. And while indulging all this virtuoso exhibitionism they casually create a breathtaking masterpiece! How clever is that? Like Hendrix taking pop-song 'Wild thing' and creating a monument out of it at Monterey. I think the band's very own sound is best revealed on 'First impressions..'<br /><br />Now the important bit is this: I think that this band is so different, unusual, unique. This is a tall order for any rock band today, because the 60s and 70s are a hard act to follow - to put it mildly! But The Strokes have accomplished this damn near impossible feat. I sincerely believe that this band is up there with the shining gleaming stellar Stones! And I personally want to thank them for reviving my belief that someone out there could write kickass rock songs and deliver them like a blow to the head. Bravo Strokes, bravo! I am missing one album and can't wait for what is to follow! I'm you fan.voice of maestro Casblancas actually an additional instrument! I now have 'Is this it' and love it too - 4 star. 0439023491|3.0|Overall I thought this was a good read. However, there are several problems that significantly hampered my enjoyment of the novel (though since it's a kids' novel, I may be judging it too harshly). Possible spoilers ahead.<br /><br />The farther I read in the book, the more I found Katniss' character to be just painfully naive. She seems oblivious to the many hints that there is a larger revolution brewing, for example the head gamemaker having the mockingjay on his watch, the incredibly obvious fact that the other tributes are colluding to protect her and peeta, etc. To me, these are so obvious, almost tropes. but Katniss consistently says thinsg like, \\"wow, I can't imagine why she would run into the fog just so me and Peeta could get away?\\" I couldn't tell whether this was a flaw in her character or just the author trying to pace the novel for younger people, who might not pick up on the hints as easily as a more seasoned reader. Nevertheless I consistently found myself groaning whenever she missed some obvious clue.<br /><br />As others have said, Katniss comes off as very selfish in this book. She has the opportunity to help everyone in the country fight back against the regime, but all she can think about is helping Peeta win the games (an obviously short-sighted solution to the problem). She tantalizingly hints at showing her contempt for the system (hanging crane, etc) but every time reverts to playing along with their games (dressing up in the coal costume for their amusement, etc).<br /><br />Also I found sending katniss and peeta back into the arena to be an incredible cop-out. Perhaps it's a shocking and suspense-laden plot development for the younger readers (who, granted, are this book's target audience), but to me it seemed not only cliche, but the worst kind of commercially driven decision. Reminds me of the endless strings of horror movie sequels that essentially just contrive some plot device to rehash the successful earlier installments (think \\"Saw\\", \\"Paranormal activity,\\" even \\"The Hangover\\"). I think Collins could have done some interesting things with it though, such as spending more time with Haymitch's backstory, or Cinna's. To me, haymitch is the most interesting, complicated character in the novel, and I think it would have really helped to know more of Cinna's motivations. Perhaps in the third book.<br /> Also the love triangle reeks of twilight emulation (and seems to have succeeded based on the swarms of what I'm assuming are teenage girls posting in the \\"Gale or Peeta\\" threads in the message boards here).<br /><br />I'll still read the third one, because it seems like the author is poised to finally cash out the revolutionary themes that she has tantalizingly hinted at for 2 novels, but my expectations are not that high.<br /><br />Take this review with a grain of salt: I realize this novel is aimed at young people. If you are a teenager or preteen who liked the first book, you will probably like this one. Or if you can keep that in mind while you read, and not sweat the cliches, you will also probably like it.<br /><br />I know my review seems harsh, but I actually did enjoy this for the most part. It's just easier to talk about the aspects that I didn't like than those I did.\\", \\"Paranormal activity,\\" even \\"The Hangover\\"). I think Collins could have done some interesting things with it though, such as spending more time with Haymitch's backstory, or Cinna's. To me, haymitch is the most interesting, complicated character in the novel, and I think it would have really helped to know more of Cinna's motivations. Perhaps in the third book.<br /> Also the love triangle reeks of twilight emulation (and seems to have succeeded based on the swarms of what I'm assuming are teenage girls posting in the \\"Gale or Peeta\\" threads in the message boards here).<br /><br />I'll still read the third one, because it seems like the author is poised to finally cash out the revolutionary themes that she has tantalizingly hinted at for 2 novels, but my expectations are not that high.<br /><br />Take this review with a grain of salt: I realize this novel is aimed at young people. If you are a teenager or preteen who liked the first book, you will probably like this one. Or if you can keep that in mind while you read, and not sweat the cliches, you will also probably like it.<br /><br />I know my review seems harsh, but I actually did enjoy this for the most part. It's just easier to talk about the aspects that I didn't like than those I did.

Making \\"Pirates of the Caribbean's\\" Keira Knightly into Sabina Spielrein didn't quite work either in my view. Sabina was attractive to Jung because she was (apart from being neurotic) very rich, very bright and very exotic. She was a Russian. She came from Rostov-on-Don, heiress to the fortune of a Russian-Jewish merchant. She spoke German with a Russian accent. Correct me if I am wrong, but Keira doesn't fit this profile, although she did best she could with her West London accent.<br /><br />Perhaps I am nitpicking. Overall, the film is good and, in cinematographic terms, good-looking. The music-score is nice. While casting is overall suspicious, Vincent Cassel is brilliant as Otto Gross, a deranged over-sexed psychoanalyst. Jung's wife is also well cast. The costumes are tremendous; the atmosphere of the times is well reproduced. I highly recommend it to everyone interested in the history of psychoanalysis and films for grown-ups.<br /><br />P.S. The main argument between Jung and Freud perhaps should have been better explained in the film: it was about nature of the libido. Freud conceived of the libido as a quantitative (or \\"economic\\") concept: it is an energy which can increase or decrease, and which can be displaced. Freud insisted on the sexual nature of the energy, and he maintained a dualism in which the libido is opposed to another (non-sexual) form of energy. Jung opposed this dualism, positing a single form of life-energy, which is neutral in character. I believe Freud was nearer the truth: at least he stayed scientific, while Jung descended into the alchemy.\\"economic\\") concept: it is an energy which can increase or decrease, and which can be displaced. Freud insisted on the sexual nature of the energy, and he maintained a dualism in which the libido is opposed to another (non-sexual) form of energy. Jung opposed this dualism, positing a single form of life-energy, which is neutral in character. I believe Freud was nearer the truth: at least he stayed scientific, while Jung descended into the alchemy.

0307888908|3.0|If you've read a few business books -- either about starting your own company, the principles of business or even about stock trading -- the Start-up of You will be of little value to you. It is very much a beginner's guide; good if you're just wetting your feet, but relatively useless if you're looking for something to elevate you/your business to another level of competition. In essence, the book tells you that it's safer to take calculated risks than remain content with the status quo, espouses the powers of networking and the importance of building transferable skills. These are all excellent points, and as a general primer, the Start-up of You is worth examining. Personally, I think that general, broad business rules work better than ultra-complex and difficult to understand ideas, so I have no problem that the tips and tricks in this book are simple. It's just that you've probably seen all of them before if you've read any other business books.<br /><br />The book goes off the rails a little bit when it resorts to anecdotal story-telling to \\"prove\\" its points. It cherry-picks a few wildly successful people (with powerful contacts) such as Sheryl Sandberg (COO of Facebook) and extrapolates that what worked for her will work for others. This type of \\"proof\\" is guilty of survivorship bias; having a never-say-die attitude, contingency plans and working your network of contacts can certainly lead to success. However, in its optimism, the Start-up of You smooths over these narratives to make its point. There are plenty of people who did exactly what Sandberg did, but failed -- I would have liked to see examples of what didn't work, as one can usually learn more from a multitude of failures than a few success stories.<br /><br />Start-up of You shares its over-reliance on rah-rah optimism and narrative fallacies with many other business book. Unusually, however, the book contains a subtle, yet constant plugging of LinkedIn, Reid Hoffman's professional networking site. While I believe the site has useful applications, it's annoying that certain parts of the book -- particularly the last portion -- devolve into the merits of networking and LinkedIn. This advertising is irritating and more than a bit insulting; purchasing a book for the privilege of being advertised to may be a clever way of generating revenue, but it is hardly endearing.<br /><br />As a starting point, this book is a nice introduction to the challenges of becoming an entrepreneur. It is about 100 pages too long and a bit too general if you've read other business books. I would recommend reading Guy Kawasaki's The Art of the Start instead, which is an excellent, well-written and relatively short resource for both beginning and experienced entrepreneurs.

0071762345|4.0|Social media networks have proven that they are the next big thing for company promotion.  They are changing the way companies communicate with their consumers and prospects.  Facebook and Twitter are here to stay.  Anyone who plans to successfully keep their consumers engaged or abreast of what their company has to offer must learn how to manage these networks.<br />Let's face it, when we see a request from someone asking to Like them on Facebook, it doesn't feel genuine. But when we ask a friend for a referral or recommendation about a product or service we take them very seriously.  Believe it or not that is what social media networking is making very easy to share with the public.<br />Dave Kerpen's  Likeable Social Media, How to Delight Your Customers, Create an Irresistible Brand and Be Generally Amazing on Facebook is definitely worth reading since his company Likeable Media is a 3-time winner of the WOMMY awards for excellence  from the Word of Mouth Marketing Association.  Yes, this is a real association.  So what's in it for the consumer to Like you on Facebook or follow you on Twitter?  According to Kerpen, \\"It's not about you, it's about your customers.  Facebook is too big to tell people to like you without telling them why. It's essential to develop that value proposition and integrate it into your communications with customers and prospects.\\"  Getting to Like is a powerful step for a company because once a customer gives their seal of approval for your company, you have access to all their friends.  Their friends will make a decision based on their Likes because they trust their friends' choices.<br />As an entrepreneur, you have to determine the value propositions you are offering to those you are asking to Like your company.  Value propositions vary from industry to industry.  A value proposition for a car dealer wouldn't be the same for a bakery.  The consumer is always asking \\"what's in it for me?\\" You have to give them a reason to Like your company.  What you are going to give, to get what you want.  Think like a consumer, not an entrepreneur.<br />Another component of the social media is that it is becoming a reliable source for learning about a company and how they value their customers. When companies respond to bad publicity via Facebook or Twitter, customers feel that the companies value them and are sincere.  \\"No human is perfect, and therefore nor is any organization. Harry Potter has influenced how we tell stories about the training of young wizards, but \\"The Night Circus\\" offers us something completely original.<br /><br />The set-up could not be more classic.  Two aging (ageless?) magicians agree to a contest - your pupil against my pupil.  A young boy and girl are thrown into a contest that seemingly lacks rules or even a forum, but it is clear that the stakes are extremely high.  Gradually, the contestants come together in the Night Circus, a legendary circus where perfect enchantment can be found, if only for a few hours, before the circus magically whisks itself off to another destination.<br /><br />Morganstern weaves a dense tale of enchantment and illusion, anchored by love.  Interestingly, she writes in the tense - so the reader is magically transported into the scene with the characters.  This little twist - it is a seemingly small matter to go from, \\"she raised her fingers to his chin\\" to \\"she raises her fingers to his chin\\" - elevates the magic of the circus and the stakes of the contest.<br /><br />While Morganstern gives us great characters, the delights of the book are found in her descriptions of the circus and its wonders - from the all-important clock to the wonders found in the various tents even down to the delightful treats for sale, the circus is a feast for the senses.  Always under control, never overblown, \\"The Night Circus\\" is a magnificent entertainment.  Check it out.

B003XF1XOQ|2.0|[No Spoilers] If you were thirsting after a satisfying and riveting finale to the Hunger Games trilogy with Mockingjay, then be prepared to continue to go hungry. Quite frankly, in this third book, Ms. Collins is the one who appears to have been brainwashed and made to forget the true essence of her first books and painfully abandon the characters she made us fall in love with. Our beloved heroine, Katniss, was demoted to a frail victim of her circumstances. What made Hunger Games so great was it's consistent theme that although Katniss was a pawn and a victim of a totalitarian government, her intuitiveness and innate sense of justice always lead her to rise above it all. She was able to win, against all odds, and that's why all of Panem, along with us readers, rallied behind her. So it was a natural expectation that in Mockingjay those qualities would be further explored and lead her to rise even more, to gain confidence in her gift for knowing the right thing to do and to grow as a character. Instead she is made into a kill-joy and not only her but ALL of the other characters as well, the exception being Finnick and really the only reason this rating earned 2 stars and not one.<br /><br />I think the positive reviewers have rebutted that the Mockingjay was true to the reality of what happens to those exposed to War but have they forgotten that the Hunger Games was fake? It was a made up fantasy world and therefore the third installment of the trilogy did not HAVE to realistically depict the emotional consequences of a war-ravaged soul. Did Ms. Collins forget her audience was \\"Young Adult\\"? That means preteens, teens and adults willing to be whisked away into a world of fantasy, justice and young love. Perhaps some silly focus group along the way told her to write Book 3 to the Video Game addicted 13 year old boy and just blow up everything in sight...they don't really want to read about friendships, relationships, or rich characters with clever voices, make sure to write about weapons a lot!!<br /><br />Another one of Ms. Collin's failures of Mockingjay was the constant repetitiveness throughout. Catching Fire fell victim to this also but by Mockingjay, it was ridiculous. Exactly how many times did the author make use of the Copy and Paste commands because just so many details of the book were ones that had already been explained numerous times before. I practically have the words to the silly Hanging Tree song memorized! If your readers are loyal then they don't need to be told again and again basics about the world of the Hunger Games. A little echoing and reflection is acceptable but when the reader finds themselves skipping over lines to get back on track with the storyline then you have failed.<br /><br />Mockingjay was also a joke when it came to the \\"Reality TV\\" spin on things. In Hunger Games and Catching Fire, I bought the whole Reality-TV-to-the-Extreme storyline completely but in Mockingjay when a full on war is being waged, giving so much of the story to the behind the scenes camera nonsense seemed to actually be mocking the original book. I couldn't take the war seriously since I was trying to be convinced that Katniss was now a pseudo-actress! I'd sooner believe taking Paris Hilton and making her the face of Occupy Wall Street! It was soooo contrived, it actually made me winch while reading and it was more annoying that the cameras were such a star of the book well until the end.<br /><br />[SPOILERS AHEAD]<br />Mockingjay Character fails:<br /><br />Katniss - as I mentioned above we fell in love with Katniss for a reason. We were given and inside view of how her mind worked, how she intuitively was able to survive based on her hunter's instincts that kept her one step ahead of the enemy. She was gutsy, beautiful yet humble. The Mockingjay version of Katniss made her wimpy, very self-absorbed with little regard for those she cared about and a whiny victim, to boot! Who'd want to be friends with her? Why not allow her to mature, time could have passed another year or two when an 18-19 yr old is truly coming to know themselves. Katniss could have realized that she really was capable of being a leader, she could have had more drive and instinct to successfully lead the rebels. Instead she was basically either a hospital patient, a depressed recluse, a failed actress or a Rambo warrior. Completely inconsistent with the Katniss we knew from Hunger Games. I miss the real Katniss and was annoyed at how she was changed so drastically in Mockingjay.<br /><br />Gale - Gale was never given a fair shot in any of the books. I fully expected Catching Fire to explore the Gale/Katniss relationship, and then Book 3 to have us on edge about the love triangle. Instead Gale was left in the peripheral of the entirety of the Hunger Games and only allowed to swoop in and save Katniss or her family from peril whenever Katniss failed or was unable to do it herself. Catching Fire hinted at their underlying love for each other but by Mockingjay it fizzled and no...it didn't \\"catch fire!\\" The poor guy was made into nothing more than a gun-slinging war strategist in Mockingjay. I guess he was smart though when he said \\"Katniss will choose whoever she thinks she can't survive without.\\" By then I think we all agreed with the spitefulness in that statement.<br /><br />Peeta - Poor guy, always the softy, always the gentleman, always the one willing to be the martyr. And yet the sweet gallant painter was turned into a tortured killing mutt! Really? For the last two books the author has been hinting at how he's willing to die for Katniss...like what a million times? So why not let him just plain DIE for Katniss?!?! Let him be the ultimate hero, not turn him into a mess of a person that just gets to fade away into the sunset baking and holding onto his mind for dear life! That's no life.<br /><br />Prim - I was happy to see Prim mature a bit, we did get to experience who Prim was a little, for a very briefmoment. And we did get to dream for a happy future for her without Hunger Games but then I hope you didn't blink because you may have missed the part where she gets blown into smithereens! That's it, nice knowing you! The author seems to have forgotten what Prim represented in Book 1 - she was the hope, the future, she was the one to protect, to fight for...she represented all of the children of Panem who were victims of the Hunger Games. If the point of the trilogy was to put an end to the Hunger Games then why, oh why, end it with killing off the one character that represented freedom from the Hunger Games! Killing off Prim now made everything else that Katniss ever did pointless. If she couldn't save Prim then what was the point?<br /><br />Katniss's Mom - Hello? Mom, are you there? She was in Book 3, right? Hmmm, I think the author kind of forgot about her. Oh well, who needs a mother when District 3 has a new nurse instead?<br /><br />Haymitch - Who would have wanted to see this brilliant but weak mentor and mastermind grow out of his dependency to the bottle and to have him had played a pivotal role as a political leader of the rebellion? Wouldn't it have been nice to see Haymitch's smarts be put to good use. Instead he too takes a backseat to the convoluted storyline of Mockingjay and also gets to fade away pointlessly into nothing, no longer anybody's mentor and basically with a lot more reasons to drink himself into oblivion!<br /><br />President Snow - Exactly what made him so evil? We never get to find out. Do we get to experience revenge for all of the treacherous acts he's done to the people of Panem? Well I guess if you count sitting him in a pretty garden and then watching him cough himself to death..or was he stepped on to death?...anyway, if you think that is satisfying vengeance then yep, I guess it counts! Bad, bad conclusion to this character, such a let down.<br /><br />President Coin - I kept picturing Hilary Clinton for some reason or maybe Isimply had to invent my own character version of her because there was so little to go on from the book. Kind of pointless character really. I expected the leader of District 13 to be someone respectable, honorable, not just a me-too President Snow. Why introduce another unlikeable enemy, we already had Snow and even he was underdeveloped.<br /><br />Finnick - I left my favorite for last, yes the only ray of sunshine in Mockingjay was Finnick. He was the Rue of Mockingjay, the one that helped Katniss and he had an interesting backstory. He was the one you rooted for to find love and hoped for recompense for what he suffered at the mercy of Snow. He was a true friend and the only character to be somewhat developed from the previous book but alas, he was not given a graceful demise like Rue. Nope, bring in the lizard mutts and quick before the reader realizes it make sure he just gets pinched off! Can I say the word \\"pointless\\" again?! I had to reread it and then I just kept asking \\"what?\\" over and over.<br /><br />52 Leegs Dead and Gone - yep that was basically the name for all of the other miscellaneous characters in this book. Boogs, Pularch,the camera guys, the other sharp shooters, the shrink, the other victors. Really nothing but collateral damage, both of the war and of this book! To think the real character development we could have experienced if we weren't caught up in keeping track of Who's Who of District 13!<br /><br />It's just a shame that readers were so invested in the Hunger Games. You had us, Ms. Collins, and yet after we bought all three of your books your finale did nothing but made us feel like we too were at the mercy of the Capitol. Now I know what it's like to be a pawn of the Hunger Games. You used the Mockingjay as a propaganda for the sale of your books rather than committing to your art.

B0011UJMK2|5.0|If all you know about Child 44 is the barest outlines of the plot, it's going to sound generic. It's a serial killer novel, one in which children are being targeted by a methodic killer, and the only one who believes that the murderers are the work of a single individual is a cop who's being given pressure from his superiors to drop the case. All of this sounds familiar, but you're missing one ingredient that changes everything, and turns Child 44 into one of the most riveting thrillers I've read in years: the fact that all of this takes place in Stalinist Russia, where even admitting that such an individual could exist would be a crime against the State, one punishable by death. And with that change in setting, everything about the novel changes. Child 44 tells the tale of an officer of the state, one whose devout faith in the system is starting to crumble, a doubt which coincides with his discovery of these child murders. But how to investigate an individual who can't officially exist, according to state dogma? And how to investigate a criminal whose motivations are not understandable for anyone looking at the case in any conventional manner? Child 44 is incredibly well-researched, immersing the reader into an incredibly oppressive world where everyone is informing on everyone else, where no one is above suspicion, and the State is greater than any individual. While the mystery aspect of the novel is never less than riveting, Smith's novel is as gripping for its portrait of a world that many of us will never understand, and his use of the environment gives the serial killer novel an entirely new feel, resulting in a book that feels like nothing else I've ever read. Moreover, here is a novel in which the basic storyline is informed constantly by the world in which it's set, meaning that every event and occurrence that happens feels organic to the tale, rather than feeling like a story that's simply sticking with a formula or going through the motions. All of this comes together to create a book whose whole is far more than the sum of its parts, creating a wholly unique tale that's part history tale, part thriller, part character study, and part police procedural, all combining in a way that feels rich and fresh, even for a seasoned thriller reader like me. An absolute knockout, on every imaginable level.omes together to create a book whose whole is far more than the sum of its parts, creating a wholly unique tale that's part history tale, part thriller, part character study, and part police procedural, all combining in a way that feels rich and fresh, even for a seasoned thriller reader like me. An absolute knockout, on every imaginable level.

B0061S0A64|5.0|I got this the first time it was offered as the FAOTD.  Back then, there were some issues with certain processors not being compatible.  That may, or may not, be the case now, but most likely all current up-to-date hardware should conceivably be compatible.<br /><br />This isn't a 'keyboard'-type musical instrument.  It doesn't 'sample' or do anything like that.  It is more along the lines of a dynamically changing piece of art that generates musical tones and notes. And it really is a fusion of art and sound.  It's beautiful in a simplistic way.<br /><br />Is it intuitive?  For some people, absolutely.  For others, including me, it is as mystifying as magnetic repulsion.  I suggest starting with the \\"ascending scale\\" layout to get a better idea of how it works and how to manipulate it.  Starting here helped me get a feel for playing and experimenting with it.  Even though I don't comprehend it as well as I should, it's still fun for me to play with, and my 5-year-old daughter really has fun with it.<br /><br />This app has more than music generation to offer since it DOES have an interactive part to it: there is a scale board on the app, from top to bottom or side to side, depending on your orientation (you can toggle this on or off); one or more fingers activates the individual bars.  You can also change and adapt the nodes or add or remove them as you see fit.  Additionally, you can change the audio settings to your preference.  Settings include attack and decay, waveform, key and so forth.  Under the settings you can enable shaking to clear the screen or press the button to clear the scree, choose portrait mode or landscape, and even set a sleep timer!<br /><br />Two more features I like are the ability to record your music, and the ability to save your current layout.  That allows for experimentation with little fear of losing your work.  Be forewarned, however, that the one feature that's missing is a selective multiple undo.<br /><br />Since the first FAOTD, the app has undergone some changes.  One of the more interesting changes is the 'gravity' mode, which allows you to turn and tilt your device to affect the notes.  It seems to also have gained a number of 'layouts' which are starting templates for getting started.<br /><br />The developer is very responsive, as I discovered the first time around.  So if you have an idea, suggestion, or an issue, you'll probably be successful in resolution or get the developer's attention for a feature or bug.<br /><br />There are no permissions to worry about except for the internet access.  It uses this, apparently, for its social media and other sharing options.  The app works just fine without internet access, so feel free to block it if it suits you.<br /><br />I can't remember if the price has come down or not; I seem to remember this being priced much higher. So let's get straight to my opinion: The book is good, but it could have been very good, if the editor had done a proper job of editing out the fluff.<br />When famous authors with a guaranteed public get power over their editors, they often begin to write longer and fluffier. Clancy began writing 400-450 pages, and ended writing 800 pages. J.K. Rowling's first Harry Potter book was her best, and among the shortest. Later on she began to fluff up the book, and no editor dared speak back to her. Apparently the same happened to Lee Child.<br />Don't misunderstand me: His descriptive power is still awesome, but this does not mean he has to describe everything. He can still turn out a page turner book-- which this book occasionally is-- but when I found myself skipping whole parts fight scenes, and of the sex scenes (!), I knew there was a problem here.<br />Now, I myself write (and publish) award-winning books (both non-fiction and fiction-- yes, thrillers), and I have always found Elmore Leonard's dictum (to cut out all the parts the reader skips) a good one. It helps keep the paying bums in the paying seats. Lee Child can still keep them, but I found my own bum over-fidgety at times. I bet others were too.<br />It would have been interesting to get the honest, confidential view of Lee Child's editor about the book. I betcha that he or she would agree that it's too long, but about 20%, perhaps even as much as 25%.<br />But other than that, it's a good page turner-- though it could have been better, if some of the beautiful lard had been peeled off, so more of the book's cheekbones could show to advantage.

B00004RITP|5.0|I happen to prefer Rattle's Bournemouth performance over the Berlin but that is not the point of this review. I am an advocate for the 10th symphony and Cooke's performing version in general. I'm of the belief that Mahler took a wrong turn in midlife which led to works starting with the Sym 5 up to Sym 8 alternately lachrymose, histrionic and saccharine. Consider the difference between the theatrical hammer blows of the Sym 6 and the dark thud of the drum in the fireman's funeral procession (as described by Mahler) in the !0th. In my opinion Mahler got back on track with Sym 9 and Das Lied von der Erde. Sym 10 continued in that same line. Mahler's early death was not only a personal tragedy but artistically cut short a wonderful renewal.  He composed much more directly whether sardonic or heartfelt.<br /><br />Although Cooke's orchestration of the 10th has been criticized, listen to the orchestration of the last movement in the Sym 9 or that of Das Lied von der Erde. They are far sparer than the preceding symphonies and orchestral songs. I may be a minority but I don't believe Mahler would have \\"thickened\\" the harmonies much more than what Cooke did although he of course would have been more inventive in counterpoint and secondary figurations. Again for the hesitant find a version of the 10th that works for you because it is Mahler.

B005QKYIZ2|5.0|Until roughly a week ago while attending my aunts wedding I knew nothing about Florence + The Machine. It wasn't until the daughter of the groom played some of this album for me did I know. And I must admit I was pretty captivated by the sound of everything right off. But it didn't hit me until actually sitting down and taking the entire album in did I realize how important this album is right now. As far as I could see,we were living in a rather non musical culture at the turn of the new millennium. It's either existed purely for entertainment or as an expression of some variety of angst for the most part. In a sense that's been a positive force: it's allowed the the individual artists to stand out more because there isn't a lot of \\"competition\\",frankly. And that's not a word I enjoy using in reference to music. Lead by singer/songwriter Florence Welch this quartet has done something very important perhaps without even realizing it. And that's free pop music from the NEED to be free.  What they do flows very organically.<br /><br />  What they do now is a little harder to explain. This albums rhythms don't come from a conventional rock n roll standpoint at all. In fact the drums rolls and thuds have a strong world beat fusion quality about them. Melodically this album is just as strong-with a mixture of styles ranging from tribal dance,soul,1980's dance music and baroque pop. Not since Rufus Wainright emerged with his debut in the late 90's have I heard such a big,melodic and diversified whole of music in one album. \\"Only If For A Night\\",\\"What The Water Gave Me\\",\\"Never Let Me Go\\",\\"No Light,No Light\\",\\"Seven Devils\\",\\"All This And Heaven Too\\" and \\"Leave My Body\\" dig right into the bigness their sound provides and doesn't let go. Of course Florence's soulful chant of a vocal instrument almost adds three other musicians alone,metaphorically. These songs are deeply romantic styled poetry-with a lot of spiritual overtones in it's outlook on life,death and speculation on the nature of both.<br /><br />  \\"Shake It Out\\" of course makes the most use of the wildly rhythmic sound of the album. \\"Never Let Me Go\\" is one of my favorites with it's orchestral pop sound of Jeff Lynne meets Bruce Springsteen. On \\"Lover To Lover\\" it's out and out uptempo,grooving soul. Again with that big production and musical back round. Another favorite of mine is \\"Spectrum\\",a well produced and lightly electronic 80's style boogie dance/funk type number. Even for that,this is definitely music made by and for human beings. Humans making music. And humans doing so with a strong and deeply passionate personality.  While I've seen people such as Bjork and others strive most of their careers for a sound like this,it's still easy to get lost in eccentricity. Somehow these Londoners manage to avoid that situation by consistently speaking directly AND poetically to those listening to their music. Already with this two albums into their career,Florence + The Machine have a long and very successful musical life ahead of them.he nature of both.<br /><br />  \\"Shake It Out\\" of course makes the most use of the wildly rhythmic sound of the album. \\"Never Let Me Go\\" is one of my favorites with it's orchestral pop sound of Jeff Lynne meets Bruce Springsteen. B007IGG0IA|5.0|Listening after hearing their previous studio set [[ASIN:B005QKYIZ2 Ceremonials]],there was a realization that I'd skimped on MTV's Unplugged series in a lot of cases. The only time I ever got involved in this was picking up Alicia Keys' own excellent [[ASIN:B000B5IPLK Alicia Keys: Mtv Unplugged]]. In fact live albums are generally a very low priority in my collection. Or were until I realized their value. It was clear upon listening to this band that they had the potential to be many different things on stage. One thing they possess is a love of theater. And not in the spectacle/drama queen sense. But the way they emotionally frame and present their music. That sense lends itself wonderfully to live performance when an artist has it. On a level beyond mere performance. Since I haven't heard this bands debut set a few of these songs are unfamiliar. But this was actually the first Florence + The Machine album I considered picking up. It was just released that day and I elected to get it with their previous studio set for my own musical needs. Overall,no regrets.<br /><br />  Basically all that's missing from this is the bigness added to their sound by studio production. It shows you how they really never needed it in the first place. A live orchestra takes the place of the studio here to provide that vast bigness in their sound. Since it opens the same way their previous album did with \\"Only If For A Night\\",that's very appropriate. \\"Drumming Song\\" is gone up mostly with rhythmic acoustic guitar. \\"Cosmic Love\\" as well as \\"Breaking Down\\",\\"Never Let Me Go\\",\\"No Light,No Light\\",\\"What The Water Gave Me\\" and \\"Shake It Out\\" all showcase that bigness via orchestration approach of this album. Florence's vocals are heard as far more overwhelming live. It's surprising she doesn't create a lot of feedback with her passionate vocal....revelries. They also interpret two standards. \\"Try A Little Tenderness\\" is still extremely soulful. But more in a stark piano based Laura Nyro way than Otis Redding's heavily gospel take on it. \\"Jackson finds them in more of a country/rock sound with a male/female duet format.<br /><br />  Even though this isn't a formal third album for Florence + The Machine,it's again another way for them to express their unique and strong talents. Again it's plain to see here they have a strong future. Not only in the studio but on stage as well. As with most live recordings,the visual element isn't there. DVD helps on that level. But the approach of the band and Florence's voice allows the listener to be imaginative and invent a visualization of their own inside their heads. An active imagination that exists outside anything commercially derived seems to be something lost in many art forms these days. And this album gives you plenty of that. It is one of those albums that probably does stretch the unplugged format,which is usually acoustic/folk oriented in nature. Than again all unplugged means is without electricity. And the fact this band had a harpist,already giving them a unique instrumental sound. Here that orchestral sound is bumped up even more of a notch. And they offer a good range of originals from their own catalog and interpretations to make this musically very strong.han Otis Redding's heavily gospel take on it. \\"Jackson finds them in more of a country/rock sound with a male/female duet format.<br /><br />  Even though this isn't a formal third album for Florence + The Machine,it's again another way for them to express their unique and strong talents. Again it's plain to see here they have a strong future. Not only in the studio but on stage as well. As with most live recordings,the visual element isn't there. DVD helps on that level. But the approach of the band and Florence's voice allows the listener to be imaginative and invent a visualization of their own inside their heads. An active imagination that exists outside anything commercially derived seems to be something lost in many art forms these days. And this album gives you plenty of that.
