B005SFS4ZU|5.0|I have never been a person that has been overly found of musicals.  I'll tell you this up front.  Basically, I could always just take 'em or leave 'em.<br /><br />Make no mistake, I am not immune to the 'songs in my head' phenomena.  Thankfully, for me, this problem has always been fairly rare.  And when it did occur, it never persisted for too long.  That is, at least it used to be this way.  Until I experienced not the 'songs in my head' problem, but rather the 'entire frickin' libretto in my exploding brain' problem.  Two very different things.<br /><br />It all started innocently enough.  Some years ago a \\"friend\\" (I now use that term loosely, as the plot was diabolical and doubtlessly premeditated.  We shall call her \\"Joyce\\".) stated that she had purchased tickets that she was to be unable to use to attend a touring company production of a play that was stopping in Sacramento (California) a city 100 or so miles away from where we both live.  It was a show that I had heard of vaguely - one that I really had no interest in seeing - a musical (wouldn't you just know it?) titled \\"Phantom-of-the-something-or-other\\".<br /><br />Right.<br /><br />I didn't want to go.  Too far.  Too much trouble.  Too much to do.  Not interested.<br /><br />My friend persisted, and as time for the performance drew near I desperately cast about for some acceptable and reasonable excuse that would allow me to gracefully bow out.  There were none forthcoming.  So, on the appointed day, I somewhat reluctantly drove off to the production with low expectations,  knowing the show was long, and hoping that I would not fall asleep whilst it was onstage.<br /><br />After arriving at the venue, and finding my seat, soon the the rock-opera music began to pound, the chandelier began to flash and my eardrums began to thrum.  With alarm, I could begin to feel the persistent tendrils of music seeking purchase in my cranium, and I found myself limp in my seat, unable to fight them off.  I began to suspect that I was surely doomed.<br /><br />But not quite yet: after the overture, a brief reprieve.<br /><br />This traveling production was presented in the Sacramento Convention Center.  As it's name implies, it is not a theatre, but - you guessed it - a convention center auditorium. On the day that I was there (I can't speak for other days when things might have been different) the acoustics were. . . less than spectacular [i. e., incredibly bad] and the show appeared to be poorly miked (or mic'ed, as you prefer).  The result was that I could hear the music fairly well - but I could not understand a single solitary word that was either spoken or sung.  I could see that the mouths of the distant performers were opening and closing, but all I could hear was muffled gibberish.<br /><br />But still, somehow, this phantom thing reached right out to this non-musical-lover and grabbed me tightly by the throat, gave me a quick shake,  and would not let me go.  I could hardly breathe. I was completely gobsmacked, even though I had pretty much no idea what the thing was about, due to my complete inability to understand any of the words.<br /><br />I think when my friend asked me afterward how I had liked it, I just stared at her with a glazed expression.  I knew that something strange and wonderful had just happened to me, but I could not for the life of me figure out exactly what it was.<br /><br />I had absolutely no idea \\"how I had liked it\\".<br /><br />I soon found out, however.  A quick trip onto the internet revealed the complete libretto, and a quick trip to the music store revealed the original cast recording, and with reading and listening the story became startlingly clear.  Within a few weeks I drove to San Francisco to see the resident theatre company of \\"Phantom\\" that was then there.  Twice. And I continued to play the CD incessantly.<br /><br />My brain began to swell, and from time to time gray matter would begin to drip out of my ears.  The entire libretto, stuck unmovable in the depths of my skull, played on and on and on. And on. I thought I might go mad.  Perhaps I did go mad for a time.  Mercifully, after some years (!) it began to quiet, and not too long after that finally it was gone.<br /><br />Freedom at last.  Or so I hoped.<br /><br />Fast forward to October 2011.  I happened to be at my local movie theatre taking in the latest drivel when an ad briefly appeared advertising \\"Phantom\\".  I felt only a minor tickle of interest, supposing it to be some quickie cheesefest, and thought to check it out on the internet when I returned home.  But by the time the drivel had ended, I had totally forgotten about \\"Phantom\\".<br /><br />My bad.  And my regret.<br /><br />Fast forward to March of 2012.  Surfing around for something to DVR one weekend afternoon, I noticed that \\"Phantom\\" was to be presented on my local PBS station.  PBS???  Phantom???  My lip curled in a sneer.  I certainly didn't need to watch THAT ever again.  For some reason I decided to DVR it anyway, sure I would never actually watch it.  Three hours long?  Good grief.  Not for me.<br /><br />Late that evening, wishing for a brief bit of escape before heading for bed, I decided to turn it on.  Just for a few minutes, you understand.  After all, it was late, I had to get up early the next morning, it couldn't possibly be any good, I just needed to unwind for a few minutes.  Smile condescendingly, maybe, at the ridiculousness of it all.<br /><br />And for the next three hours I was totally and completely lost.<br /><br />I have read other reviews that I would like to briefly rebut, before I go on.<br /><br />Basically these other reviewers state that POTO_25 is \\"pretty good, but can never match a live performance\\".  To that I say, with all due respect: \\"Sir! You are very much mistaken!\\"  My three experiences with live POTO were nowhere near the quality of POTO_25.  NOT EVEN CLOSE.<br /><br />The Sacramento performance was as described above.  Flawed, yet the brilliance of the piece still shone through.  On my first visit to POTO at the Curran theatre in San Francisco, they announced that \\"Christine\\" was ill, and that an actress had been flown in from Los Angeles for that evenings performance.  Well, that particular \\"Christine\\" was just fine - unfortunately, the rest of the cast, particularly  the Phantom, sounded like they all had a very bad,  sniffly colds.  On my second visit to the Curran, the  Phantom was suddenly a low baritone, that would fudge on the tenor notes.  I do not say that these performances were bad, because they were not.  They were good.  However, they were somewhat less than wonderful.  And no where close to amazing.<br /><br />Which brings me, finally, again, to the amazement that is POTO_25.<br /><br />After seeing the show previously several times, I knew what to look for as POTO_25 began.  The point that will tell me whether this will be a great and amazing performance, or merely a good and serviceable one.  For me, that point is when I first hear the Phantom's voice, immediately after Christine's \\"Think of Me\\" triumph.  We hear the Phantom softly calling her name.  I have heard this calling when I could not understand the words. I have heard this calling when the Phantom had the sniffles.  I have heard this calling when the Phantom was a low baritone, and should have been a tenor.<br /><br />I had never heard it the way I imagined it could - and should - be sung. Until that moment.  I sat straight up on the couch and threw my clock right out the window: O.  M. G.<br /><br />This is a musical whose implausible story is hung on the thinnest of gossamer threads.  The amazing thing is not that it works - and in the case of POTO_25 it works overwhelmingly and brilliantly well - but that it works at all.  With this show, as it comes to show time, all of the elements are in place:  the costumes, the sets, the music, the effects.<br /><br />It only remains for the actors to give it life.<br /><br />Andthey do here flawlessly.<br /><br />Wendy Ferguson (Carlotta):<br /><br />What a revelation.  The previous Carlotta's I had seen had all seemed to be thrown in as some kind of a diversion, to make time as the other characters were introduced.  I am watching this and suddenly I'm thinking:  What the. . .?  Is Carlotta making eyes at the male ballet dancer?  Indeed she was!<br /><br />And her voice.  Oh my.  Like no Carlotta I have ever heard.  And the wonderful characterization continues throughout the  the show.  What before had always seemed to me to be a throw-away part, is now a full-blooded and critical member of the ensemble.  She is funny.  She is poignant.  She is brave.  She makes one care about what happens to her.  All with wonderful acting and amazing vocals.<br /><br />Hadley Fraser (Raoul):<br /><br />A very different - and welcome - take on the part.  In previous \\"Phantoms\\" Raoul has always seemed to be a bit of a wishy-washy rich boy, with no real backbone.  It never really made sense to me that he falls so instantly in love with Christine.<br /><br />Here he doesn't.<br /><br />Mr. Fraser is telling here a different tale with his acting, maybe not so in love - but worried about his investment.  Watching his facial expressions (and it is glorious to be able to actually see facial expressions!) one notices that he is carefully calculating.  He does not believe - at first - Christine's assertions about her \\"Angel of Music\\".<br /><br />And when she later professes her love for him on the roof of the opera, he seems a bit surprised and even taken aback.  And seems not to be really in love with her at all - but only leading her on in order to get her back inside to the paused performance.<br /><br />After all, he would likely lose his investment in the Opera Populaire should she not return to the performance, and need to refund all of those tickets.  This Raoul is a strong, assertive and calculating man - not a sniveling love-struck boy. I like him!  Wonderful and absorbing tenor vocals.<br /><br />Sierra Boggess (Christine):<br /><br />What can one say of perfection?  Her bravura performance takes ones breath away.  I have never heard this part so well sung before, nor have I ever seen it so well acted, even sitting in the 5th row at the Curran.  Frightened, happy, despairing.  Soprano vocals unmatched, and unmatchable.  Bravo Ms. Boggess!<br /><br />Ramin Karimloo (The Phantom):<br /><br />Well, here we finally come to the crux of it.  The make-or-break performance of the show.  So let me state this up front, without wasting any more time: I have never heard - nor seen acted - a better Phantom, nor do I ever expect to hear - or see acted - one at this level of performance art ever again in my lifetime.<br /><br />Period.<br /><br />The casting of Mr. Karimloo in this part puts a whole different take on the story.  When he first comes onstage, after his offstage vocals, I was very surprised.  I mean, what's this, a young guy?  He is intense, virile, commanding - and seems unstoppable.  And yet, at the proper times, he is also soft, retiring, poignant, and despairing.<br /><br />When he is onstage, there is no one else there.  His youth and vitality completely commands and changes the love triangle of the show.  Always before, the Phantom has generally been played by an older man - that is, a man much older than is Christine.  An older man moves differently and reacts differently.  It is always supposed that a young man (in this case Raoul) will always win the love of the beautiful young woman over the affections of this older man.<br /><br />This time we are not so sure.  With a young Phantom, and particularly with *this* young Phantom, we realize  that he really does have a chance with her.  This adds a dramatic tension that has, until now, been missing.<br /><br />As I have written above, I have seen this show more than once, but at the end of POTO_25 Mr. Karimloo's Phantom  so had me in his grip, and so understanding of his loss, that in the final scene I dissolved into tears.  This had never happened to me before when viewing this show.<br /><br />Vocally, from the lowest baritone notes to the highest tenor, Mr. Karimloo navigates this torturous score with ease, elegance and confidence.  Just when one thinks he can't possibly stretch out a high note or a low note one second longer (even he must finally run out of breath!), he ends with a sweet and extended vibrato.<br /><br />My only complaint is that when he was singing the video would often cut to a reaction shot of Christine.  I didn't want to watch Christine.  I only wanted to look at him.<br /><br />I can't wait to hear what this man does next.<br /><br />BRAVO Mr. Karimloo!!!!<br /><br />[Sidebar:  as I don't follow theatre in general, and musical theatre in particular, Mr. Karimloo's name was unfamiliar to me.  In the interest of  fairness, I decided to Google him, and found a few YouTube interviews.  What I discovered was a very handsome young man, extremely soft-spoken, polite and self-effacing.  Where was the big ego?  Where was the big voice?  There obviously is some mistake here.  Ramin: call me and let's discuss.]  ;-)<br /><br />To conclude, I will say that this will without doubt prove to be the definitive version of \\"Phantom of the Opera\\" for years to come.  If you want to watch \\"Phantom\\", you really can't do better than this.  It is an asset to any DVD/Blu Ray collection, and a \\"must have\\" for those who love Andrew Lloyd Webber's masterpiece.<br /><br />So, you may be asking, is it over for me now, the Music of the Night?  No.  I don't think it will ever be over again.  At least not for me.<br /><br />There is no more hope of summertime.<br /><br />Because the Phantom of the Opera is HERE: inside my mind.<br /><br />And the music plays on, and on.<br /><br />And on. . .<br /><br />May God have mercy on my soul.they do here flawlessly.<br /><br />Wendy Ferguson (Carlotta):<br /><br />What a revelation.  The previous Carlotta's I had seen had all seemed to be thrown in as some kind of a diversion, to make time as the other characters were introduced. B0049WHNLO|5.0|The 1956 Newport Jazz Festival put Ellington back on top solely because of the 14:56 performance of Diminuendo And Crescendo In Blue (with 27 choruses by Paul Gonsalves on tenor saxophone) and, aided in large part, by a beautiful blonde woman whose dancing inspired the crowd nearly as much as the music. Suffice to say that it ranks as one of the great moments in jazz and a highlight of Ellington's career.<br /><br />To be honest, there are studio segments mixed in with the live performance on this particular album. This is because of technical issues, including Gonsalves playing into the wrong microphone, with yet another mic capturing the audience and their energy. This was rectified in a 1999 reissue. Regardless, this album does capture the moment in all of its magical glory, and when combined with [[ASIN:B0048Y5KPY Duke Ellington And The Buck Clayton All-Stars At Newport]], which captures some other highlights of the same concert, you have a pretty good audio recreation of what transpired. If you are a purist, hard core fan or historian, then you will probably want to purchase this album instead: [[ASIN:B0013DDOCO Ellington At Newport 1956 (Complete)]].
0743497465|4.0|Another fast-paced thriller dealing with the NSA from Dan Brown. I found this novel to be right on par with Digital Fortress and thought that it was a very well-written thriller. From start to finish Deception Point takes the ball and runs with it like any other Dan Brown thriller. I like that the plot moves around in scenery from the Arctic to Washington, DC and a few points in-between keeping the action flowing and the characters moving around instead of being static in one place. The characters are well written and believable and, in this book, Brown has written another very strong female lead character as he also did in Digital Fortress.<br /><br />I don't know why these two novels didn't get the press that the Robert Langdon novels did, perhaps it's because they deal with the NSA instead of having a religious based theme that stirs up controversy. Whatever the reason, if you enjoy Dan Brown's Robert Langdon novels or are just a fan of a good thriller, I'd recommend giving Deception Point a chance.

1400069289|4.0|Why are habits so hard to break?  Why did I just grab that handful of nuts?  The answer, according to recent studies described in Charles Duhigg's \\"The Power of Habit...\\"  is that  habits are programmed through repetition of a \\"cue / routine / reward\\" cycle  into a primitive section of the brain known as the basal ganglia.  The basal ganglia  is distinct from the sections that control memory and other high-level thought, and our capacity to override our habits through higher level thought is limited.  It is possible, however, to \\"reprogram\\" habits, notably by altering the routine while leaving the cue and reward unchanged.<br /><br />Thus ends the very satisfying first third of the book.  Most of the remainder is a based on a much broader definition of the word \\"habit\\" that includes routines, corporate processes, values, and more.  The book uses the \\"cue / routine / reward\\" cycle to explain many of these more broadly-defined habits, though it leaves me wondering whether that cycle really applies in this broader sense.  Starbucks for example is lauded for teaching employees a routine to console an angry customer, and Duhigg  calls that routine a habit.  Perhaps habit is an acceptable word, but not in the sense of the first third of the book; I imagine that a Starbucks employee consoling an angry customer is fully engaging  higher brain functions.  Consoling an an angry customer isn't the same as grabbing a handful of nuts.  There  is a lack of continuity in the way Duhigg uses the term \\"habit\\", and a more transparent approach to this would have helped.<br /><br />The above criticism aside, I found the book engaging and thought-provoking.  I did not have to \\"muscle through\\" any part of it, which is rare for me for non-fiction.  My only decision is now how many stars to dock the book based on my criticism; I will make that decision once I fortify myself with another handful of nuts.

B005NF2UE6|5.0|What are all these comments I've been reading about Nickelback selling out? Are you kidding me? Here and Now is a hard rocker at the very core of what Rock n Roll is supposed to be: a commentary and celebration of humanity. Song by song analyses get in the way of seeing the forest for the trees.<br /><br />From the opening riffs of \\"This Means War\\" Hear and Now rocks hard. What is the subject of the war in the song? Well I don't know, but as a protest song or as a fight in a relationship it works just as well and suggests that going into the fight it's not possible to win. Social commentary or the fallout of a relationship, it doesn't matter, the song is hard hitting and demands philosophical reflection.<br /><br />Bottoms Up? A defiant rocking party drinking song.<br /><br />When We Must Stand Together: On first listen, an average uplifting socially conscious rock ballad with pithy lyrics: \\"When we could feed a starving world With what we throw away But all we serve are empty words That always taste the same.\\" But what makes this song truly brilliant is its construction. From the opening drum beats, this song drives hard and the listener is caught up in the flow because the drum beats so strongly, just like war drums that drive our society or the clock that turns every single day that keeps us moving in our daily routines where we don't pause to reflect or to care on what is really happening in this world. The key to this song are the lyrics<br />\\"The right thing to guide us<br />Is right here inside us<br />No one can divide us<br />When the light is nearly gone<br />But just like a heartbeat<br />The drumbeat carries on...\\"<br />But how many of us listen to what our heart dictates and do what's right when the drums are keeping us moving in our comfortable daily routines? This song screams at us to listen to our hearts and break free of society's drums.<br /><br />Midnight Queen, Gotta Get Me Some, and Everything I Wanna Do are pure, unadulterated songs of attraction, lust, and unabashed sexuality--in the spirit of the very heart of rock n roll, which was from the beginning a euphemism for sex. Everything I Wanna Do and Midnight Queen are so explicit they would probably make your parents blush. That the songs are hard driving and lyrically creative only adds to their brilliance.<br /><br />Lullaby, Holding on to Heaven, Trying Not To Love You and Don't Ever Let It End are standout tracks as mature rock ballads and clearly show that Nickelback are caring and sensitive individuals beneath their hard hitting rocker veneers. Trying Not to Love You, like Someday before it, illustrates that the wrong focus in a relationship is to dwell on the things that create division. If there is attraction, if there is a connection, if there is a spark then energy should be spent focusing on the positives and what it takes to make the relationship work. Every relationship has a silver lining and that is what holds the relationship together. So often people want to get divorced and quit working in their relationships. Nickleback again suggests that path only leads to pain and that by embracing the positives in relationships can conflict be resolved and happiness obtained.<br /><br />Nickleback are adult men with adult wants and needs: sex, intimacy, great times with friends. But they also care about the world they live in and sing about social activism, consciousness, the phoniness in our plastic society--all adult themes. That they can do this on Here and Now with brilliant song construction, passionate and earnest vocal delivery, great guitars and driving drums while steering clear of cheesiness--even on When We Stand Together--is a testament to their brilliance as artists.

0312641893|5.0|I'm having a bit of trouble starting this review, because I just can't properly put into words how much I loved this book. Seriously. I was hesitant to get on the bandwagon, so to speak, and read Cinder because I wasn't sure if it was going to deliver on its premise. I mean, Cinderella as a cyborg? Lunar people planning an Earthen invasion? It was almost as if Cinder was tailor-made for me. Which is also why I was even more apprehensive to try it - I didn't want my hopes getting raised so high, only to be left disappointed and frustrated that the book didn't turn out as I expected.<br /><br />Cinder, though? Completely amazing. It absolutely delivered, and I'm hitting myself on the noggin' now for not reading it sooner, and experiencing the awesomeness that is Cinder.<br /><br />Cinder is a cyborg mechanic (and the best one, at that) living in New Beijing where a new plague called leutomosis is ravaging through its citizens. She is blamed and sent away by her stepmother to become a test subject in finding a cure after her stepsister Peony contracts the disease. But Cinder gets more than she bargained for, and suddenly, Cinder finds the fate of Prince Kai, the citizens of the Eastern Commonwealth, and the rest of the Earthen districts in her hands.<br /><br />So...what exactly did I love about Cinder?<br /><br />Well, to begin with - Cinder. Cinder was a fantastic heroine. I loved how well-developed and complex her character was. She was strong, intelligent, competent, and loyal to those who were loyal to her. She had her flaws as well - she was socially inept (and really, when you're marginalized, abused, and maltreated by your only remaining relatives, well, she could've become much more than socially inept) and almost uncharacteristically meek in front of her stepmother sometimes, but I think those qualities served to make her into an even more well-rounded character. I just love how she never lost sight of what was important. She was persistent and courageous, even at the expense of her safety. She definitely became one of my favourite YA heroines ever.<br /><br />World-building. The world-building in Cinder is admittedly at an introductory level. I believe there's going to be four books in the series, so it makes sense that not all of the backstory or details were presented in the first book. However, the world-building I did see, I absolutely loved. I loved, loved, loved that the novel was set in New Beijing and the East. In so many YA sci-fi or dystopian books I read, it's as if the rest of the world (nevermind the world, heck, the rest of the USA) ceased to exist. No China, no India, no Australia - nothing beyond the good ol' USA. But Cinder is different, and thank goodness for that. I loved how the author gave small glimpses (and okay, they are SMALL, but they're there, nonetheless) of what the world in Cinder's time would've looked like. The USA, Canada, and most of NA and SA amalgamated into one country with several ruling provinces called The American Republic. There was also the European Federation, the African Union, the United Kingdom, Australia, and of course, Cinder's own country - the Eastern Commonwealth. Like I mentioned, they're minute details, yes, but the fact that they're mentioned makes my inner international law and political science geek smile.<br /><br />The Romance. I loved how understated the romance was between Cinder and Prince Kai. It was natural and the progression didn't seem forced at all. It didn't feel out of place and it didn't overtake the main premise of the story. It was just the right amount, and for once, I'm looking forward to how the romance between the two characters will develop. I'm glad that the author chose the slow and steady road when it comes to Kai and Cinder, because it makes their interactions so much more meaningful and realistic. No insta-love here, folks, and thank goodness for that.<br /><br />Secondary Characters. Sometimes secondary characters can come off as very stiff and cardboard-like. But not in Cinder. From Cinder's stepsisters to the Prince's advisor - they were all well-developed and thought out. I absolutely loved Iko, Cinder's android! Her quips had me giggling, and I found myself wishing I had my own Iko, too. I also liked that Cinder had at least one ally in her household - Peony. She was gentle and kind, and seeing her fall ill with the plague broke my heart. And of course, there's Kai, the Prince. I enjoyed the chapters from his POV, and I liked that he wasn't brash or overly confident. He had doubts about his ability to lead, and he was apprehensive about taking over the throne. But despite all of that, he had the well-being of his people at heart, and he was willing to make personal sacrifices for the safety and continued existence of his people.<br /><br />Aliens. Well, okay, the people of Luna (the Moon) were technically not aliens as they were ultimately humans who chose to colonize the Moon centuries before the start of the novel. There's not much known about the people of Luna and their Queen yet apart from their ability to wield bioelectricity (though, I'm going to go with Cinder and just call that magic) and use glamours to essentially brainwash and control the minds of those they encounter. I'm hoping, however, the book two changes this and expands more on their culture, world, and \\"magic\\".<br /><br />With that said, what didn't I like about Cinder?<br /><br />I had to think long and hard about this, and really, I think the only thing that I wasn't really a fan of was the Cinderella elements itself. I never really thought I'd ever say that because I'm more than a little bit obsessed about fairy tales and fairy tale retellings, but I think that Cinder could've stand on its own without the fairy tale element. In fact, I would almost wager on to say that Cinder probably would've been better without the Cinderella aspect. It just didn't seem to go with the overall plot of the book, and really only served as a small \\"gimmick\\".<br /><br />Still, Cinder was amazing. It was a fantastic mix of mystery, intrigue, and romance wrapped in a cloak of science fiction with elements of a fairy tale we've all come to love and adore.<br /><br />In a nutshell: Get it and read it. Now. You won't regret it.tiff and cardboard-like. But not in Cinder. From Cinder's stepsisters to the Prince's advisor - they were all well-developed and thought out. Very much so.<br /><br />I spent a fair amount of time thinking about it after I saw it.<br />Having not read the book, I was free to think about how their<br />society could become as it is. The story became believable to me,<br />in the sense I could extrapolate from our society today, to the<br />future the movie presents.<br /><br />Because the characters live in a much different world than we do,<br />a world different than other fictional worlds, I didn't immediately<br />appreciate them, nor the acting. But the characters, and the acting,<br />became multi-dimensional after I had some time to integrate things.<br /><br />The characters all belong in their world, they have a very personal<br />stake in it, and they are all trying to preserve the values it holds<br />for them personally, whether they are \\"good guys\\" or \\"bad guys\\",<br />innocent or worldly. They all have hopes, and they all have fears.<br />No one is truly secure, and the ending, although quiet, is very stark.<br />Yet very human also.<br /><br />The movie has a mix of young actors, and middle aged actors, and<br />that mix works superbly, yet subtly, when they have 1-on-1 scenes<br />together. They bring out the best in each other. First impressions<br />fall by the wayside, because these characters are all much more than<br />they seem. They live in a complex world, dangerous for the powerful<br />and powerless alike, and they are all desperately trying to wend their<br />way thru it alive. Several of them are keenly focused on the humanity<br />of the people around them, trying to keep that in the forefront, or<br />at least bring it into view for a few moments in the brief time they<br />have left.<br /><br />I debated giving this 4 stars or 5. I'm often tempted to try making<br />comparisons with other movies, but for Hunger Games, I choose not<br />to do that. I decided on 5 stars, because this movie makes the most<br />of what it has. A thought-provoking story, sufficient budget, a lot<br />of very talented people, and a long enough run-time, 2:20, to present<br />itself as it truly is. A very, very good movie.

0439023521|5.0|I was so convinced that I wouldn't enjoy this book that it sat on my e-reader for weeks until my daughter brought home a hard copy and literally had to twist my arm to read it. Sci-fi is not usually my favourite genre, and I can probably count on one hand the amount of futuristic novels I have truly enjoyed reading. But Suzanne Collins' \\"Hunger Games\\" totally proved all my prejudices wrong. The story was so well crafted and the characters so engaging that I was hooked from page 1, and had a massive late night read-a-thon until I had finished the whole book, as I could not put it down.<br /><br />What I particularly liked about this book is that Collins has created a futuristic world which bears enough similarities to our own so that I could relate to the things she described without having to put in too much effort. The character Katniss tells about her world without offering too many tedious explanations that would have made the book a chore to read (like many other sci-fi novels) - instead, she takes her dysfunctional world for granted and accepts its injustices and hardships just as we get blind to the faults in our own backyard. This to me added to the feeling of reality to the story rather than trying to find explanations for the reasons why her world was the way it was, as seen through the eyes of a 16-year old girl.<br /><br />To put it very simply, the story itself played out like a cross between Survivor meets Gladiator meets Big Brother, but with much more depth and action and of a much superior calibre. Who has not questioned our ability to watch horrible reality TV images 24/7 whilst eating our dinner? So it was no big stretch to imagine the people of Panem doing just that, on a much larger scale. I especially liked Katniss' and Peeta's ability to remain true to their own moral guidelines throughout the book without appearing to be sickening goodie-two-shoes, or without preaching about right and wrong - let the readers draw their own conclusions. I also liked that there were no defined villains in this story, rather victims of circumstance all trying to stay alive - this again made the story a lot more realistic and believable.<br /><br />To cut a long review short, I loved this book. I can usually tell whether a book truly grabbed me from the feeling of unreality when a phone-call or a visitor interrupts the reading and you surface bleary eyed and disorientated, still under the spell of the imaginary world you have just inhabited. Yes, Zappa ridicules hippie culture with satire and he also criticizes mainstream society and police violence, but I don't find any of it to be particularly clever. Just irritating. And smug. Most of the songs here amount to musical snippets that don't last very long, which would be a blessing if they didn't immediately segue into yet another irritating song. Zappa's singing (or whoever else is doing it), when he's not simply talking, is sometimes monotonous and off key.<br /><br />The thing I find most irritating is this attitude that comes across through the words and the music itself (especially the way he uses the xylophone) that seems to be saying it thinks that it's the most funny, most clever thing ever made. Lines like, \\"You paint your head/ Your mind is dead/ That's yooOOOUU... American womanhood!\\" are delivered with this \\"nya-nya-nya-nya-boo-boo\\" quality that actually makes me want to smack the singer. I admit that there are a few segments that have genuine merit from a musical perspective (for example, the piano parts on \\"Absolutely Free\\"), but it hardly matters because the presentation is so atrocious. The music may be melodic at times but much of it is quite simplistic and repetitious. Some of the studio effects might sound kinda cool but in this context they just sound cheesy.<br /><br />Other annoying things about WE'RE ONLY IN IT FOR THE MONEY: Zappa's engineer periodically whispers heavily-echoed narcissistic nonsense throughout the album...croaking frog noises are often heard...sped-up chipmunk voices...backwards guitar and other effects that merely sound like a child goofing off in the studio. Whether or not all this is supposed to be some sort of aural commentary on psychedelic pretensions, such a notion certainly doesn't make it worth listening to. This being Zappa, there's some disgusting scatological \\"humor\\" to be found, both petulant and babyish. The song \\"Let's Make the Water Turn Black\\" has lines like, \\"wizzing and pasting and pooting through the day\\" and, \\"all the while on the shelf in the shed/ Kenny's little creatures on display.\\"  \\"Ronnie saves his `noomies' on a window in his room/ A marble to be seen/ dysentery green.\\" If kiddie gross-out humor is your thing then Zappa is definitely for you.<br /><br />The album ends with \\"The Chrome Plated Megaphone of Destiny,\\" over six minutes of tuneless, utterly worthless musique concrete featuring a heavy dose of chipmunk laughter. Why WE'RE ONLY IN IT FOR THE MONEY has been called one of the greatest albums ever by sources like Rolling Stone magazine is completely beyond me. I'm utterly baffled. Zappa actually made some good music and he was an incredible guitarist but I contend that this record is complete and utter trash.<br /><br />Rating: STAY AWAYse throughout the album...croaking frog noises are often heard...sped-up chipmunk voices...backwards guitar and other effects that merely sound like a child goofing off in the studio. 006197806X|5.0|This review contains spoilers for the first book, Delirium.<br /><br />SUMMARY -<br />Lena Holoway is old, dead, and buried.  Lena Morgan Jones is new, reborn, and ready to fight at whatever the cost.  The memory of Alex, of Grace and her mother, are all she has left of her life before.  Her new life is with Raven and Tack, after a grueling time in the Wilds; she lives in Brooklyn, New York, where she is a student, a cured, a Zombie.  She has been charged with trailing Julian Fineman, son of prominent leader of the Deliria Free America.  When the two are thrown into an adventure they never bargained for, they must put aside their differences - and fight a forbidden love that can never be fostered.<br /><br />MY THOUGHTS -<br />I read. I cried. I threw a fit.<br /><br />This book makes me want to pull a squirt gun on the publishers, Dick Harper style, and yell, \\"GIVE ME YOUR ARC'S! GIVE ME YOUR ARC'S!\\"<br /><br />I've said it before and I'll say it again.  It's something that never gets old, and I don't believe it will ever change.<br /><br />Lauren Oliver is Brilliant.  With a capitol \\"B\\".<br /><br />And because of that Brilliancy, we have Before I Fall, Delirium, and now Pandemonium.  (I'm sure Liesl & Po is just as wonderful, but I have yet to read it.)  If I had to pick a favorite between Delirium and Pandemonium, I'd pick the first book, but its sequel is so excellent I cannot deny my love for it.  It is a Forever Favorite and will stay there for all time.<br /><br />Once again - Lauren's writing is freaking GORGEOUS.  It envelops you in warm, comforting arms and feeds you inspiration by the spoonful.  Even after the story is over, it sticks with the reader for a long, long time.<br /><br />CHARACTER NOTES -<br />Lena Morgan Jones is my new hero.  She's my role model.  If I could be half as brave as this young woman, then I'd feel like a superhero.  In Delirium, Alex is the one who causes her to bud into a beautiful, brave young woman.  Pandemonium shows the continued growth, the building of a new Lena who strives to do right by her memory of Alex, of her mother.<br /><br />Now, ahem, ALEX.  No, he is not exactly in this book as a character, but his memory shines for him, real and encouraging - to the reader as well as to Lena.  I loved the way Lena's thoughts of him, her motivation to make her life exactly what he'd died for her to have, practically brought him to life.  Oh, how I miss him.  I'm serious - I felt like I was Lena, aching for him, wishing for his strong guidance, but also pushing forward, fighting for another day, to live, breathe love.  That is why I love Alex.<br /><br />Julian Fineman.  Can I just ask a question?  HOW THE HECK DID YOU BECOME WONDERFUL AS WELL?!?!?!  After gaining composure...  Julian is...wow.  I wasn't sure of him at first, but now I'm dead certain.  What I'm certain of (besides his awesomeness) I have no idea.  I just hope Lauren has something awesome in store for him.  (Although, compared with Alex....he doesn't even compare.  Ok, so, we're not comparing him with Alex right now.  Sorry, I just can't help myself.)<br /><br />Raven, Tack, Hunter, Sarah, etc. - these are the men and women and children of the wilds.  They were all very clear and precise and consistent.  I mean, come on, these are Lauren's characters I'm talking about here - what else would they be?!<br /><br />STORY NOTES -<br />I'm going to be honest:  I missed 37 Brooks, Back Cove...Portland.  I really did.  But here, in Pandemonium, Lauren Oliver has built something just as real, just totally different - Brooklyn, NY.  An entirely new setting, a new structure, a strong difference in her MC - and yet everything felt as though I'd experienced it my whole life.  So natural, life-like.<br /><br />I really liked the then and now idea - how they were different times but they still melted into each other, seamless.  Lena's contrasted self was a beautiful thing to see, to be reminded of.<br /><br />Wanna know what really blowsme away?  The love triangle.  Yep, you heard me.  I flipping love this love triangle.  It's not giggling teenage girls with hunky men where all they want to do is make out.  It's something totally different, out-of-this-world, mindboggling: it's mature.  Yes, Lauren Oliver has succeeded in creating the mature and realistic love triangle I've ever read.  Now, I have my biases.  I'm a \\"first love\\" fan 1,000,000%.  But honestly, I trust Lauren's expertise.  What she says goes.  I'm placing my already fragile heart in her hands.  I trust she won't crush it.  (Ok, just kidding.  She's already crushed it and shredded it and mercilessly pounded it into the dirt a million times but...you get what I'm trying to say, right?)<br /><br />On the topic of love, I must say the love story in this one was a bit less believable, because of who Lena is and what she's been through.  I mean, with Delirium's slow-progressing romance, thick and delicious and wondrous, and with the world's best lit-man, nothing really compares now.  Because of this, Pandemonium's romance seemed quite a bit fast.  It was definitely beautiful, and still believable, but not as much as Lena and Alex's.  I sure do hope this makes sense...<br /><br />And then...WE ALL DIE BECAUSE OF THAT END.  WHY?!?!?!!  My mind seriously threatens to burst every time I even think about it because I can't even wrap my head around Lauren's brilliance!!!!  She is the Queen of Cliffhangers and Heartbreakers.  WHY do we have to wait till freaking NEXT YEAR to know what happens?!?!?!<br /><br />It's just....pure genius.  Pure.  Genius.<br /><br />SUMMING IT UP -<br />You heard me - pure genius.  From first page to last.  Every word in between.  Everything.  I'm seriously freaking out right now!!<br />Requiem:  I need you desperately, but you are worth the wait!!!<br /><br />For the Parents -<br />A handful of s**t, brief strong language (2 F's, 2 GD's).  Reference to \\"unnaturals\\" (two guys are apparently a couple).  A male character asks to see Lena without a shirt on.  She covers her breasts but he does get a peek at the rest.
